Oaths on Movement: Rulings and Principles
Series of rulings on oaths related to movement and actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for five common oath scenarios involving leaving, coming, and riding and explain the legal reasoning behind each ruling.

Core Idea
An oath is broken when the oath-bearer causes the prohibited act; incidental acts during a permitted exception do not break the oath.

In this passage, the Hidayah presents a series of case studies on oaths related to movement. Each example reveals a distinct legal principle: commanding someone to carry you out breaks an oath not to leave; performing an additional task during a funeral does not break an oath if the funeral was the primary purpose; an oath to go to a place remains binding until death; giving permission for one exit does not cover subsequent exits; and the context of an oath determines whether a specific action constitutes a violation. We will parse each case to understand the Hanafi position on causation, incidental actions, and contextual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
The Mosque Case
A man swears he will not leave the mosque; then he commands another man to carry him out. Identify that the oath is broken because his command caused the leaving. Note: Hanafi law holds the oath-bearer liable for actions he initiates through others.
The principle: Causation (tasabbub) is equivalent to direct action in oaths.
2
The Funeral Case
A man swears he will not leave his house except for a funeral. He leaves for a funeral and then does another task. The oath is not broken because the primary purpose was permitted; the incidental action does not violate.
The principle: Incidental acts during a permitted exception are waived.
3
The Basra Case
A man swears he will definitely go to Basra but never goes and then dies. He becomes a breaker at the moment of death. The oath remains binding until death; ability to fulfill is assumed.
The principle: Oaths are not voided by eventual impossibility; breach occurs at the last moment of opportunity.
4
The Wife's Permission Case
A husband swears his wife will not go out without permission. He gives permission once, she goes out, then later she goes out without permission. The oath is broken because each departure is a separate act; permission does not carry over.
The principle: Permission for one act does not imply ongoing permission.
5
The Breakfast Case
A man tells someone 'Sit and take from me,' then swears if he has breakfast his slave is free. He goes home and has breakfast. The oath is not broken because the breakfast referenced in the oath was likely the shared meal with that person, not any breakfast.
The principle: Oaths are interpreted according to the context and apparent intent of the speaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الخروج والاتيان والركوب وغير ذالك
قسم کھائی کہ مسجد سے نہیں نکلے گا پھر ایک آدمی کو حکم دیا اس نے اٹھا کر باہر کر دیا حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی کہ اپنے گھر سے جنازے کے علاوہ کیلئے نہیں نکلے گا پھر جنازہ کیلئے نکلا پھر دوسرا کام بھی کر لیا حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی کہ بصرہ ضرور بضر ور آؤں گا پھر نہ آیا یہاں تک کہ فوت ہو گیا تو اپنی زندگی کے آخری لمحات میں حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی کہ میری بیوی میری اجازت کے بغیر باہر نہیں نکلے گی اسے ایک دفعہ اجازت دے دی وہ باہر نکلی پھر دوبارہ اجازت کے بغیر باہر نکلی حانث ہو جائے گا
ایک آدمی نے کسی کو کہا اجلس فتخد عندی اس نے کہنا اگر میں ناشتہ کروں تو میر اغلام آزاد پھر اپنے گھر کی طرف گیا اور ناشتہ کیا حانث نہیں ہوگا
Chapter on oaths regarding leaving, coming, and riding and other such things. He swore an oath that he will not leave the mosque, then he commanded a man who lifted him and took him out: he becomes a breaker of the oath. He swore an oath that he will not leave his house except for a funeral, then he left for a funeral and then also did another task: he does not become a breaker of the oath. He swore an oath that he will definitely come to Basra, then he did not come until he died: he becomes a breaker of the oath in the last moments of his life. He swore an oath that his wife will not go out without his permission; he gave her permission once, she went out, then she went out again without permission: he becomes a breaker of the oath. A man said to someone, 'Sit and take from me'? He said, 'If I have breakfast, my slave is free,' then he went to his house and had breakfast: he does not become a breaker of the oath.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on oaths related to movement and actions. The first ruling: if a man swears he will not leave the mosque, but then he commands someone to carry him out, he breaks his oath because his action caused the leaving, even though he didn't physically exit himself. The second ruling: if he swears he will not leave his house except for a funeral, and he leaves for a funeral but then also does another task, he does not break the oath because the primary purpose was the funeral; the additional action is incidental. The third ruling: if he swears he will definitely go to Basra but dies before going, he becomes a breaker at the moment of death, as the oath remains binding until his last breath. The fourth ruling: if a husband swears his wife will not go out without his permission, and he gives her permission once, she goes out, then she goes out again without permission, he breaks the oath because each departure is a separate occurrence; the permission is not perpetual. The fifth ruling: a man says to someone, 'Sit and take from me' (perhaps offering food), and then swears that if he has breakfast his slave is free; he goes home and has breakfast. He does not break the oath because the breakfast referred to in the oath was probably a specific meal with that person, not his own breakfast at home.

Key Terms
قسم (qasam): An oath (yamin) invoking Allah or a divorce/manumission as a condition.
حانث (hanith): One who breaks an oath; the state of being in violation of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears he will not leave the mosque, then he orders someone to carry him out, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he caused the leaving
B) No, because he did not physically leave
C) No, because he used a proxy
D) Yes, but only if he intended to break it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths on movement are broken when the oath-bearer causes the prohibited act, and incidental actions during a permitted exception do not violate the oath.

Oaths on Eating and Drinking: Interpreting Terms by Custom
Collection of rulings linking oath wording to breach conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a specific act breaks an oath by applying the Hanafi principle of customary ('urf) interpretation to terms used in oaths.

Core Idea
In oaths, words are taken in their customary sense; a specific term covers only its immediate category.

When someone swears an oath about eating or drinking, the precise wording matters. This lesson explores how Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between similar items (like fresh vs. dried dates, meat vs. roasted meat) and how impossible oaths are treated. Understanding 'urf (custom) is key to correctly judging oath breaches.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exact wording of the oath
Carefully read the oath phrase. Note each term used, such as 'meat', 'fruit', 'date', 'wheat', etc. The specific wording triggers the ruling.
E.g., 'I will not eat meat' vs. 'I will not eat roasted meat'.
2
Determine the customary meaning of the key term
Apply the local custom ('urf) to understand what the term commonly refers to. For example, 'fruit' (fākiha) in the Hanafi school typically excludes items like cucumber and gourd.
If custom varies, the default is the general usage of the time and place.
3
Check if the consumed item falls under that customary meaning
Compare the item actually eaten or drunk with the customary definition. If it is a different category (e.g., busr vs. rutab), the oath is not broken.
Transformed states (raw vs. cooked, fresh vs. dried) are often considered distinct.
4
Recognize that transformed or prepared forms are separate categories
A rule from the passage: an oath on 'flour' includes bread made from it (since it's a direct product), but an oath on 'meat' does not include 'roasted meat' (since it's a different culinary item).
Similarly, an oath on 'wheat' is not broken by eating it without chewing.
5
Apply the principle to impossible oaths
If someone swears to do something impossible (e.g., ascend to the sky), the oath is still contracted and breaking it requires kaffarah. The impossibility does not nullify the oath; rather, the swearer is held to his word.
This shows that oaths are binding even for improbable acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الاكل والشرب
قسم کھائی کہ اس کھجور سے نہیں کھاؤں گا اس سے کھجور کا پھل مراد ہے۔
قسم کھائی کہ اس بچے سے یا جوان سے کلام نہیں کرے گا پھر بوڑھا ہونے کے بعد کلام کیا حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی کہ بسر نہیں کھائے گا پھر رطب کھالیں حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی کہ رطب نہیں خریدوں گا پھر بسر کا خوشہ خرید لیا جس میں رطب بھی تھیں حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی گوشت نہیں کھاؤں گا پھر چھلی کا گوشت کھا لیا حانث نہیں ہوگی
مسائل
قسم کھائی چربی نہ کھائے گا نہ خریدے گا کونسی چربی مراد ہوگی
قسم کھائی کہ اس گندم کو نہیں کھاؤں گا چبائے بغیر کھانے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی اس آٹے سے نہیں کھائے گا پھر اس کی روٹی کھالی حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی بھنا ہوا نہیں کھائے گا گوشت مراد ہوگا
قسم کھائی کہ سر نہیں کھائے گا سر کا اطلاق سر پر ہو گا
قسم کھائی کہ فاکہ نہیں کھائے گا پھر انگور، انار، ترخرما، لکڑی، کھیرا کھایا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی کہ ادام سے نہیں کھاؤں گا ، ادام کا مصداق
ناشتہ نہ کرنے کی قسم اٹھائی غذا کا اطلاق کون سے کھانے پر ہوتا ہے
اگر کیا ان لبست او اکلت او شربت فعبدی حر پھر کہا میری یہ مراد ہے قضاء تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی
قسم کھائی کہ دجلہ سے نہیں پیے گا پھر برتن سے پی لیا حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی ان لم اشرب الماء الذي في هذا الكوز اليوم فامراته طالق اور کونے میں پانی نہیں تھا حانث نہیں ہوگا
اگر قسم کھائی ليصعدن السماء يا ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهبا قسم منعقد ہو جائے گی اور حانث ہو گا
Chapter on oaths regarding eating and drinking. He swore an oath that he will not eat from this date: the fruit of the date palm is meant. He swore an oath that he will not speak with this child or youth, then spoke after he became old: he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not eat unripe dates (busr), then ate ripe dates (rutab): he does not become a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not buy ripe dates, then bought a bunch of unripe dates in which there were also ripe dates: he does not become a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not eat meat, then ate roasted meat: he does not become a breaker. Issues: He swore an oath that he will not eat fat nor buy it: which fat is meant? He swore an oath that he will not eat this wheat: eating without chewing does not make him a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not eat from this flour, then ate bread made from it: he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not eat roasted: meat is meant. He swore an oath that he will not eat head: the word 'head' applies to the head. He swore an oath that he will not eat fruit, then ate grapes, pomegranate, fresh dates, gourd, cucumber: he does not become a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not eat 'udam' (relish): what constitutes 'udam'? He swore an oath not to have breakfast: what food does 'ghidha' apply to? If he said, 'If I wear, or eat, or drink, then my slave is free,' then he said, 'I intended such and such,' his word is not accepted in court. He swore an oath that he will not drink from the Tigris, then drank from a vessel: he does not become a breaker. He swore an oath, 'If I do not drink the water that is in this pitcher today, then my wife is divorced,' and there was no water in the pitcher: he does not become a breaker. If he swore an oath, 'Surely I will ascend to the sky,' or 'Surely I will turn this stone into gold,' the oath is contracted and he becomes a breaker.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This chapter collects rulings on oaths about specific foods and drinks. The general principle is that the meaning of a term in an oath is determined by common usage (urf) and the intent of the swearer. For example, if one swears not to eat from a particular date, it means the fruit itself, not the palm. If one swears not to speak with a child, and the child grows old, speaking to him breaks the oath because the identity remains. Distinctions between fresh and dried dates (busr and rutab) are considered different varieties, so an oath on one does not cover the other. Similarly, meat and roasted meat are considered separate; an oath on meat does not apply to roasted meat. However, an oath on flour includes bread made from it because bread is a form of prepared flour. For an oath on 'fruit' (fakiha), only items commonly called fruit in the local custom are covered; in the Hanafi view, items like grapes, pomegranates, fresh dates are fruit, but gourd and cucumber are not, hence no breach. The passage also addresses cases where the oath is impossible (e.g., ascending to the sky) – the oath is still valid and the swearer breaks it because he voluntarily swore to something impossible; he must pay kaffarah. Also, if a condition is based on a non-existent object (e.g., water in a pitcher when it is empty), the condition is impossible, so the oath is not broken.

Key Terms
بسر (busr): Unripe dates (still hard).
رطب (rutab): Fresh, ripe dates (soft).
فاکہ (fākiha): Fruit in common parlance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears he will not eat meat, and then he eats roasted meat, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because roasted meat is a type of meat
B) No, because roasted meat is considered a different category
C) No, because he intended raw meat
D) Yes, but only if he also eats raw meat

Key Takeaway
The meaning of terms in oaths is determined by common custom ('urf), not by technical definitions.

Oaths on Speaking: Hanafi Rulings and Parsing Key Cases
Rulings on oaths regarding speech and communication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify different oaths regarding speech and determine when they are broken based on Hanafi criteria.

Core Idea
An oath about speech is broken by intentional conversation, but not by Quran recitation in prayer; ambiguous terms are interpreted by custom.

Oaths concerning speech are common in daily life, but their precise scope requires careful parsing. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on what counts as 'kalam' (conversation) and what does not, with special consideration for acts of worship and ambiguous expressions. In this lesson, we will break down the key scenarios from the classical text to understand when an oath is breached.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core subject of the oath
First, determine whether the oath is about speech (kalam) in general, or specifically about speaking to a named person, or conditional on another event. This sets the scope.
E.g., 'I will not speak to Zaid' vs 'I will not speak unless he permits'.
2
Assess the act that allegedly breaks the oath
Examine whether the act performed is considered 'kalam' in the Hanafi sense. For instance, reciting Quran in prayer is not kalam; writing or gestures may also differ.
Key exception: worship acts are exempt.
3
Check the knowledge and condition of the listener
In some oaths, the listener's state matters (e.g., sleeping). The Hanafi view holds that speaking to a sleeping person still breaks the oath because the speech occurred, regardless of response.
The oath is about the speaker's action, not the listener's receipt.
4
Handle conditional oaths and ambiguous time expressions
If the oath is tied to a condition (e.g., 'unless so-and-so arrives'), the condition must be fulfilled to avoid breach. For words like 'a while' or 'many days', custom defines the minimum.
Custom ('urf) is the default interpreter in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الكلام
قسم کھائی کہ فلاں سے کلام نہیں کروں گا پھر کلام کی کہ وہ سن رہا تھا مگر وہ سویا ہوا تھا حانث ہو جائے گا اور اگر قسم کھائی فلاں کی اجازت کے بغیر کلام نہیں کروں گا اس نے اجازت دے دی اور اسے معلوم نہیں اس نے کلام کیا حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم اٹھائی لا يكلمه شهرا قسم اٹھانے کے وقت سے مہینہ شمار ہوگا
اگر قسم کھائی کلام نہیں کرے گا پھر نماز میں قرآن پڑھا حانث نہیں ہوگا
قسم کھائی فلاں شخص سے گفتگو کروں تو میری بیوی کو طلاق تو بیوی کو کب طلاق ہوگی
اگر کہا ان كلمت فلانا الا ان يقدم فلان يا حتى يقوم فلان يا الا ان يأذن فلان يا حتى يأذن فلان فامرأته طالق فلاں کے قدوم اور اذن سے پہلے کلام کی حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی کہ لا يكلم عبد فلان اور کس معین غلام کی نیت نہیں کی یا فلاں کی بیوی یا فلاں کے دوست کے ساتھ کلام نہیں کر دوں گا۔ مذکورہ الفاظ کے ساتھ قسم اٹھانے کا حکم کسی معین غلام پر قسم کھائی یاکسی معین بیوی کیسا تھ یا کسی معین دوست کیا تھے کلام نہیں کروں گا ، غلام میں حانث نہیں ہو گا عورت اور دوست میں حانث ہو جائے گا۔
قسم کھائی لا يكلم صاحب هذا الطيلسان اس نے چادر کو فروخت کر دیا پھر کلام کی حانث ہو جائے گا
قسم کھائی لا يكلم حینا اوز مانا کتنا زمانہ مراد ہوگا ؟
قسم اٹھائی لا يكلم الدهر اس سے کتنے دن مراد ہوں گے؟
قسم کھائی چند دنوں تک بات نہیں کروں گا کتنے دن مراد ہوں گے؟
غلام کو کہا ان خدمتی ایام كثيرة فانت حر ، ایام کثیرہ کا مصداق کتنے دن ہوں گے؟
Chapter on oaths regarding speech. He swore an oath that he will not speak to so-and-so, then he spoke while he was hearing but he was asleep: he becomes a breaker. And if he swore an oath that he will not speak without so-and-so's permission, he gave permission but he (the speaker) did not know, then he spoke: he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath, 'I will not speak to him for a month': the month is counted from the time of swearing. If he swore an oath that he will not speak, then recited Quran in prayer: he does not become a breaker. He swore an oath, 'If I speak to so-and-so, my wife is divorced': when does the divorce occur? If he said, 'If I speak to so-and-so unless so-and-so arrives, or until so-and-so stands, or unless so-and-so permits, or until so-and-so permits, then my wife is divorced,' if he speaks before the arrival or permission, he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not speak to so-and-so's slave, and he did not intend a specific slave, or the wife of so-and-so, or the friend of so-and-so: the ruling for these expressions? If he swore an oath on a specific slave, or a specific wife, or a specific friend, that he will not speak to them: for the slave, he does not become a breaker; for the woman and the friend, he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not speak to the owner of this cloak; then he sold the cloak, then he spoke: he becomes a breaker. He swore an oath that he will not speak for a while or a time: how long is meant? He swore an oath that he will not speak forever: how many days are meant? He swore an oath that he will not speak for a few days: how many days are meant? He said to his slave, 'If you serve me for many days, you are free': how many days constitute 'many days'?
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This chapter deals with oaths concerning speech. The first ruling: if a man swears not to speak to someone, and he speaks while that person is hearing, even if the person is asleep, he breaks the oath because the speaking occurred and was heard. However, if he speaks without knowing that the other has given permission, he still breaks the oath because the condition was fulfilled. In cases where the oath is for a specific period, the month is counted from the moment of swearing. Notably, reciting the Quran in prayer is not considered breaking an oath because it is a form of worship, not ordinary conversation. When the oath is conditional on another event (like someone's arrival), the condition must be fulfilled to avoid breach; speaking before that condition makes the oath broken. For oaths regarding specific individuals: if one swears not to speak to so-and-so's slave without specifying a particular slave, he is not considered to have broken it if he speaks to any slave, because the reference is unclear. But for a specific wife or friend, the oath is binding. Also, if one swears not to speak to the owner of a cloak, and then the cloak is sold, the owner remains the same person (the seller), so speaking to him still breaks the oath. The passage also addresses ambiguous time durations: 'a while' (hin) is interpreted by the court, but generally it means a short unspecified time; 'eternity' (dahr) is understood as a year in some interpretations, but here the text leaves it open. 'Many days' (ayyam kathira) is defined by custom; some scholars say a minimum of three days, but it varies.

Key Terms
کلام (kalām): Speech or conversation; in this context, spoken words.
حین (ḥīn): A while; an indefinite short period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man swears not to speak to someone, and then he recites Quran loudly in prayer while that person is present, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he spoke
B) No, because Quran recitation is not considered conversation
C) No, because he was praying
D) Yes, but only if he intended to break the oath

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh treats oaths on speech with precision: only ordinary conversation breaks the oath, and exceptions like Quran recitation and ambiguous terms are resolved by custom and context.

Fractional Manumission from Overlapping Ambiguous Statements
Intra-Hanafi dispute over partial manumission from ambiguous statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the fractional manumission shares for each slave in a scenario with two overlapping ambiguous statements and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf from Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Two ambiguous statements on overlapping groups of slaves create fractional freedom: the permanently present slave gets three-quarters, the others get half or quarter.

Manumission is a weighty act in Islamic law, freeing a slave and removing the master's ownership. But what happens when a master makes ambiguous statements like 'one of you is free' to changing groups of slaves? This passage from the Hidayah presents a classic Hanafi dispute over how to apportion freedom when the groups overlap, illustrating the school's meticulous approach to speculative fractions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slaves and the two statements
List the three slaves (A, B, C) and the two statements: first to A and B, second to A and C after B leaves and C enters.
No note
2
Apply the principle of shared freedom from each statement
Each statement 'one of you is free' creates a shared entitlement to one whole slave among the addressees, split equally. First statement: A and B each get half of a slave's freedom. Second statement: A and C each get half, but A already has a share.
Hanafi principle: ambiguous manumission among multiple slaves creates a share for each.
3
Calculate fractions considering overlapping claims
A has half from first statement. The second statement gives another half, but it overlaps with A's existing half. To resolve, A gets half of his remaining half (quarter) from second statement, totaling three-quarters. B gets only first half (half total). C gets half from second statement according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but only quarter according to Muhammad because A's existing half absorbs half of the second statement's potential share.
The difference hinges on whether the second statement's half is split equally or adjusted for prior ownership.
4
Compare the two rulings
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: A 3/4, B 1/2, C 1/2. Muhammad: A 3/4, B 1/2, C 1/4. The practical difference: C's legal status (half free vs. quarter free) affects his ability to marry, earn, and be inherited.
No note

باب عتق احد العبدين
بسم الله الرحمن الرحیم
باب عتق احد العبدين
ترجمہ باب دوغلاموں میں کسی ایک کے آزاد ہونے کا بیان
ایک آدمی کے تین غلام ہیں دو مولیٰ پر داخل ہوئے مولیٰ نے کہا کہ تم میں سے ایک آزاد ہے ایک نکل گیا اور دوسرا آ گیا مولیٰ نے کہا تم میں سے ایک آزاد ہے اور پھر مر گیا اس کا حکم وَمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ ثَلَثَةُ أَعْبُدِ دَخَلَ عَلَيْهِ اِثْنَانِ فَقَالَ اَحَدٍ كُمَا حُرِّثُمَّ خَرَجَ وَاحِدٌ وَدَخَلَ آخَرُ فَقَالَ أَحَدُ كُمَا حُرِّثُمَّ مَاتَ وَلَمْ يُبَيِّنْ عِتَقَ مِنَ الَّذِي أَعِيْدَ عَلَيْهِ الْقَوْلُ ثَلَثَةُ اَرْبَاعِهِ وَ نِصْفُ كُلّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الْأَخَرَيْنِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَ أَبِي يُوْسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ كَذَالِكَ إِلَّا فِي الْعَبْدِ الْآخَرِ فَإِنَّهُ يُعْتِقُ رُبُعُهُ أَمَّا الْخَارِجُ فَلَاتَ الْإِيْجَابَ الْأَوَّلَ دَائِرٌ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الثَّابِتِ وَ هُوَ الَّذِى أُعِيدَ عَلَيْهِ الْقَوْلُ فَاَوْجَبَ عِتْقَ رَقَبَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا لِإِسْتِوَائِهَا فَيُصِيبُ كُلَّا مِنْهُمَا النِّصْفُ غَيْرَانَّ النَّابِتَ اسْتَفَادَ بِالْإِيْجَابِ الثَّانِي رُبَعًا أَخَرَلَانَّ الثَّانِي دَائِرَبَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الدَّاخِلِ فَيَتَنَصَّفُ بَيْنَهُمَا غَيْرَانَّ النَّابِتَ اسْتَحَقَّ نِصْفَ الْحُرِّيَّةِ بِالْإِيجَابِ الْأَوَّلِ فَشَاعَ النِّصْفُ الْمُسْتَحَقُّ بِالنَّانِي فِي نِصْفَيْهِ فَمَا أَصَابَ الْمُسْتَحَقَّ بِالْاَوَّلِ لَغَاوَمَا أَصَابَ الْفَارِغَ بَقِيَ فَيَكُونُ لَهُ الرُّبْعُ فَتَمَّتْ لَهُ ثَلَثَةُ الْأَرْبَاعِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أُرِيدَ هُوَ بِالثَّانِي يَعْتِقُ نِصْفُهُ وَلَوْارِيدَ بِهِ الدَّاخِلُ لَا يَعْتِقُ هَذَا النِّصْفُ فَيَتَنَصَّفُ فَيَعْتِقُ مِنْهُ الرُّبُعُ بِالثَّانِي وَالنِّصْفُ بِالْأَوَّلِ وَأَمَّا الدَّاخِلُ فَمُحَمَّدٌ يَقُولُ لَمَّا دَارَ الْإِيجَابُ الثَّانِي بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الثَّابِتِ وَقَدْاَصَابَ الثَّابِتَ مِنْهُ الرُّبْعُ فَكَذَالِكَ يُصِيبُ الدَّاخِلَ وَهُمَا يَقُولَانِ إِنَّهُ دَائِرَ بَيْنَهُمَا وَقَضِيَّتُهُ التَّنْصِيفُ وَإِنَّمَانَزَلَ إِلَى الرُّبْعِ فِي حَقِّ الثَّابِتِ لاسْتِحْقَاقِهِ البَصْفَ بِالْإِيجَابَ الْأَوَّل كَمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَ لَا إِسْتِحْقَاقِ لِلدَّاخِل مِنْ قَبْلُ فَيَثْبُتُ فِيهِ النِّصْفُ ترجمہ اگر کسی شخص کے (الف، ب، ج) تین غلام ہوں۔ اتفاق سے ایک مرتبہ دو غلام (الف، ب ) اس کے سامنے آئے تو اس نے یہ کہا کہ تم دونوں میں سے ایک آزاد ہے۔ اس کے بعد ان دونوں میں سے ایک (الف) وہیں رہا اور ایک (ب) وہاں سے نکل گیا۔ اس کے بعد وہ تیسرا (ج) بھی آ گیا اس وقت پھر مولیٰ نے کہا تم میں سے ایک آزاد ہے۔ اس کے بعد اس کی کچھ وضاحت کئے بغیر ہی مر گیا۔ تو امام ابوحنیفہ رحمتہ اللہ علیہ وامام ابو یوسف رحمتہ اللہ علیہ کے نزدیک جو غلام برابر موجود رہا (الف) یعنی مولی کے قول کا وہ دوبار مخاطب ہوا اس کے ۳ ۴ تین چوتھائی حصے آزد ہوں گے اور باقی دونوں ( ب اور ج ) میں سے ہر ایک کا آدھا آدھا آزاد ہوگا اور امام محمد رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے اسی طرح کہا ہے سوائے آخر میں آنے والے (ج) کے کہ اس کا صرف چوتھائی حصہ آزاد ہوگا۔ واضح ہو کہ پہلا قول حاضر رہنے والے (الف) اور نکل جانے والے (ب) کے درمیان خطاب تھا۔ اس لئے اس کلام سے ایک غلام آزاد ہوا جو ان دونوں میں مشترک ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ دونوں برابر کے مستحق ہیں۔ لہذا ہر ایک کو آدھی آدھی آزادی ملی یعنی نکلنے والے (ب) میں سے آدھا اور حاضر رہنے والے (الف) میں سے آدھا آزاد ہوا اور وہ جو موجودرہ گیا تھا (الف) وہ تیسرے آنے والے (ج) کے ساتھ ایک غلام کا مستحق ہو کر آدھا پائے گا۔ لیکن اسے صرف چوتھائی حصہ اس لئے ملا کہ موجود میں سے پہلے قول کی وجہ سے
Chapter on freeing one of two slaves. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Chapter on freeing one of two slaves. Translation: Explanation of the chapter on the freeing of one of two slaves. A man has three slaves; two entered upon the master. The master said, 'One of you two is free.' Then one left and another entered. The master said, 'One of you two is free.' Then he died without clarifying. The ruling: According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the slave who remained present (i.e., the one addressed twice) has three-quarters of him freed, and half of each of the other two. Muhammad said likewise except for the last entering slave; he said only a quarter of him is freed. As for the one who left: the first address was between him and the one who remained, making a full slave's freedom shared equally, so each gets half. But the one who remained gained an additional quarter from the second address, because the second address was between him and the newcomer, so it is split between them. However, the one who remained already had half from the first address, so the second addresses's possible half overlaps with his existing half; only the portion that falls on the empty half (the other half) counts, giving him another quarter, totaling three-quarters. If the second address was intended for him, he would get half; if for the newcomer, he gets nothing; so it's split. Thus from the second he gets a quarter, plus the half from the first, total three-quarters. As for the newcomer, Muhammad says since the second address is between him and the one who remained, and the one who remained already got a quarter from it, similarly the newcomer gets a quarter. But Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is between them and the principle requires splitting in half. The reduction to a quarter for the one who remained is because he already had half from the first, and the newcomer had no prior claim, so he gets half. Translation: If a person has three slaves (A, B, C). Once two slaves (A, B) came before him and he said, 'One of you is free.' Then one of them (A) stayed and one (B) left. Then the third (C) came, and the master said again, 'One of you is free.' Then he died without clarifying. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, the slave who stayed (A), who was addressed twice, has three-quarters freed, and each of the other two (B and C) has half freed. Imam Muhammad said similarly except for the last one (C); he said only a quarter of him is freed. It is clear that the first statement was between the one who stayed (A) and the one who left (B). That statement freed one whole slave shared between them, so each got half. Then the one who stayed (A), in the second statement with the newcomer (C), is entitled to half of another whole slave, but since he already had half from the first, the second half is partially overlapping. The calculation yields him only a quarter from the second, plus a half from the first, total three-quarters. The newcomer gets half according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf because the second statement is split between him and the stayer who already has half, so the newcomer gets half. But Muhammad gives him only a quarter because the stayer's prior half absorbs part of the second.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on freeing one of two slaves (or more precisely, freeing one of a set) presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over a specific case involving three slaves and two ambiguous statements by the master before his death. The case: A master has three slaves (A, B, C). He says to two of them (A and B) 'one of you is free.' Then B leaves and C enters. The master then says to the new pair (A and C) 'one of you is free,' then dies without clarifying. The question is what fraction of each slave is freed. The positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: A (the one present both times) is 3/4 free; B (the one who left) is 1/2 free; C (the newcomer) is 1/2 free. Muhammad: same except C is only 1/4 free. The reasoning: The first statement is between A and B, so one whole slave is freed between them, each gets 1/2. The second statement is between A and C, so another whole slave is freed between them, giving each an additional 1/2. But A already has 1/2 from the first; if he gets 1/2 from the second, that would make him fully free, but the second statement might have been intended for C instead. So to resolve the ambiguity, A gets half of the second share only on his remaining half (i.e., 1/4), totaling 3/4. For B: he only participated in the first statement, so 1/2. For C: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say since the second statement is between A and C equally, and A already has 1/2 from before, C gets half of that statement (1/2). Muhammad says because A already has 1/2, the second statement's share is split between A's existing half and C, so only 1/4 goes to C. The text provides the detailed logical reasoning for each position.

Key Terms
عتق (i'tq): Manumission: the act of freeing a slave, which renders the slave free and removes the master's ownership.
مشترك (mushtarak): Shared: in this context, the freedom of one whole slave being shared proportionally among multiple slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case described, what fraction of slave C (the newcomer) is free according to Imam Muhammad?
A) One-half
B) Three-quarters
C) One-quarter
D) Fully free

Key Takeaway
When ambiguous manumission statements overlap on changing groups, freedom is apportioned fractionally, with the slave addressed multiple times receiving the largest share, and the newcomer either half or quarter depending on the Hanafi authority followed.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Partial Manumission of Slaves with Multiple Statements
Complex intra-Hanafi dispute on partial manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the freedom shares for slaves when a master makes two ambiguous manumission statements, and explain the difference between the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
When a master says 'one of you is free' twice with changing slaves, the calculation of shares differs due to overlapping rights.

This passage presents a sophisticated intra-Hanafi dispute on calculating partial manumission when a master makes two ambiguous statements. The disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other revolves around how the second statement's effect is distributed, especially for the slave who enters only for the second statement. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to apply the principles of overlapping rights in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three slaves
The master has three slaves. Two enter, he says 'one of you is free'. One exits, a third enters, he says again 'one of you is free'. He dies without clarifying. Label the three slaves: al-kharij (exited after first statement), al-thabit (stayed both times), and al-dakhil (entered for second statement).
The key is that the first statement is between al-kharij and al-thabit; the second between al-thabit and al-dakhil.
2
State the positions of the two groups
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: al-thabit gets 3/4, al-kharij and al-dakhil each get 1/2. Muhammad: same except al-dakhil gets 1/4 instead of 1/2.
The difference is only on al-dakhil's share.
3
Follow the reasoning for al-thabit (the stayer)
From the first statement, al-thabit and al-kharij share one full freedom: each gets 1/2. From the second statement, one full freedom is shared between al-thabit and al-dakhil, but al-thabit already has 1/2. The second half spreads: half of that half (1/4) goes to his unfree portion, giving him 3/4 total.
The prior half from the first statement causes the second half to be effectively quartered.
4
Analyze the dispute over al-dakhil (the entrant)
Muhammad argues that since the second statement is confined between al-thabit and al-dakhil, and al-thabit gets only 1/4 from it, al-dakhil should also get 1/4. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the confinement requires halving by default; al-thabit's reduction is due to his prior right (not applicable to al-dakhil), so al-dakhil gets the full 1/2.
The dispute is about whether the reduction to 1/4 for al-thabit is a feature of the statement itself or a special circumstance.
5
Apply the calculation to marad al-mawt (death illness)
If the statements were made during death illness, manumission is treated as a will and limited to one-third of the estate. The total shares of freedom according to Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf are 7 (3 for al-thabit, 2 each for the others). These are proportionally reduced to fit within the third.
The calculation shows how the dispute affects practical outcomes in inheritance.

أُعِيدَ عَلَيْهِ الْقَوْلُ ثَلَاثَةُ أَرْبَاعِهِ وَنِصْفُ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الْآخَرَيْنِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ. وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ كَذَلِكَ إِلَّا فِي الْعَبْدِ الْآخَرِ فَإِنَّهُ يُعْتِقُ رُبُعُهُ. أَمَّا الْخَارِجُ فَلِأَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ الْأَوَّلَ دَائِرٌ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الثَّابِتِ وَهُوَ الَّذِي أُعِيدَ عَلَيْهِ الْقَوْلُ فَأَوْجَبَ عِتْقَ رَقَبَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا لِاسْتِوَائِهِمَا فَيُصِيبُ كُلًّا مِنْهُمَا النِّصْفُ غَيْرَ أَنَّ الثَّابِتَ اسْتَفَادَ بِالْإِيجَابِ الثَّانِي رُبُعًا آخَرَ لِأَنَّ الثَّانِيَ دَائِرٌ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الدَّاخِلِ فَيَتَنَصَّفُ بَيْنَهُمَا غَيْرَ أَنَّ الثَّابِتَ اسْتَحَقَّ نِصْفَ الْحُرِّيَّةِ بِالْإِيجَابِ الْأَوَّلِ فَشَاعَ النِّصْفُ الْمُسْتَحَقُّ بِالثَّانِي فِي نِصْفَيْهِ فَمَا أَصَابَ الْمُسْتَحَقَّ بِالْأَوَّلِ لَغَا وَمَا أَصَابَ الْفَارِغَ بَقِيَ فَيَكُونُ لَهُ الرُّبْعُ فَتَمَّتْ لَهُ ثَلَاثَةُ الْأَرْبَاعِ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أُرِيدَ هُوَ بِالثَّانِي يَعْتِقُ نِصْفُهُ وَلَوْ أُرِيدَ بِهِ الدَّاخِلُ لَا يَعْتِقُ هَذَا النِّصْفُ فَيَتَنَصَّفُ فَيَعْتِقُ مِنْهُ الرُّبْعُ بِالثَّانِي وَالنِّصْفُ بِالْأَوَّلِ. وَأَمَّا الدَّاخِلُ فَمُحَمَّدٌ يَقُولُ لَمَّا دَارَ الْإِيجَابُ الثَّانِي بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ الثَّابِتِ وَقَدْ أَصَابَ الثَّابِتَ مِنْهُ الرُّبْعُ فَكَذَلِكَ يُصِيبُ الدَّاخِلَ وَهُمَا يَقُولَانِ إِنَّهُ دَائِرٌ بَيْنَهُمَا وَقَضِيَّتُهُ التَّنْصِيفُ وَإِنَّمَا نَزَلَ إِلَى الرُّبْعِ فِي حَقِّ الثَّابِتِ لِاسْتِحْقَاقِهِ النِّصْفَ بِالْإِيجَابِ الْأَوَّلِ كَمَا ذَكَرْنَا وَلَا اسْتِحْقَاقَ لِلدَّاخِلِ مِنْ قَبْلُ فَيَثْبُتُ فِيهِ النِّصْفُ. قَالَ فَإِنْ كَانَ الْقَوْلُ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَرَضِ قُسِّمَ الثُّلُثُ عَلَى هَذَا وَشَرْحُ ذَلِكَ أَنْ يُجْمَعَ بَيْنَ سِهَامِ الْعِتْقِ وَهِيَ سَبْعَةٌ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا لِأَنَّا نَجْعَلُ كُلَّ رَقَبَةٍ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةٍ لِحَاجَتِنَا إِلَى ثَلَاثَةِ الْأَرْبَاعِ فَنَقُولُ يَعْتِقُ مِنَ الثَّابِتِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَسْهُمٍ وَمِنَ الْآخَرَيْنِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا سَهْمَانِ فَيَبْلُغُ سِهَامُ الْعِتْقِ سَبْعَةً وَالْعِتْقُ فِي مَرَضِ الْمَوْتِ وَصِيَّةٌ وَمَحَلُّ نِفَاذِهَا الثُّلُثُ. ترجمہ: ... (اردو شرح) ... تشریح: ... فائدہ: ...
Whoever has three slaves, two enter upon him, and he says, 'One of you is free,' then one goes out and another enters, and he says, 'One of you is free,' then he dies without clarifying. Then from the one who was addressed again, three-quarters becomes free, and half of each of the other two, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Similarly, except for the last entering slave, that only one-quarter becomes free. As for the one who went out: because the first statement is confined between him and the one who stayed, who is the one addressed again, so it necessitates the freedom of one slave between them due to equality, so each gets half. But the one who stayed benefits from the second statement another quarter, because the second statement is confined between him and the entering one, so it is divided between them into halves. However, the one who stayed already deserved half-freedom by the first statement, so the half deserved by the second statement spreads over his two halves; what falls on the half already deserved by the first is null, and what falls on the empty half remains, so he gets the quarter, thus completing three-quarters for him. And because if he were intended by the second statement, his half would become free, and if the entering one were intended, this half would not become free; so it is halved, so one-quarter becomes free by the second and one-half by the first. As for the entering one, Muhammad says: Since the second statement is confined between him and the one who stayed, and the one who stayed got from it one-quarter, so likewise the entering one gets one-quarter. But they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say: It is confined between them, and its requirement is halving. It only reduced to a quarter in the case of the one who stayed because he already deserved the half by the first statement, as we mentioned, while there is no prior right for the entering one, so the half is established for him. He said: If the statement was from him during illness, the third is divided according to this. The explanation: The shares of freedom are combined, which are seven according to their statement, because we make each slave into four parts due to our need for three-quarters. So we say: The one who stayed becomes free of three shares, and from each of the other two, two shares, so the shares of freedom reach seven. And manumission during death illness is a will, and its place of execution is the third.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya presents a complex intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the manumission of slaves when a master makes two ambiguous statements. The scenario: A master has three slaves. Two enter, he says 'one of you is free'. One exits, a third enters, he says again 'one of you is free'. He dies without clarifying. The calculation of freedom shares differs between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other. The passage first states the positions: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the slave who was present both times (al-thabit) gets three-quarters free; each of the other two get half free. According to Muhammad, the same except the slave who entered second (al-dakhil) gets only one-quarter free. The reasoning is then detailed. For the slave who exited first (al-kharij), the first statement was between him and the one who stayed, so each gets half of a slave's freedom (since one whole slave's freedom is split). For the stayer, the second statement is between him and the entrant; normally half each, but since the stayer already has half from the first statement, the half from the second statement spreads over his two halves (one half already free, one half not). The part that falls on the already-free half is wasted, the part on the unfree half gives him an additional quarter. So total: half + quarter = three-quarters. Alternatively, if the second statement intended the stayer, his remaining half would be freed; if it intended the entrant, this half would not be freed; thus half of that half (quarter) is freed by the second statement. For the entrant, Muhammad argues that since the second statement was between him and the stayer, and the stayer got only a quarter, the entrant similarly gets a quarter. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the second statement requires halving between the two; the stayer's reduction to a quarter is due to his prior right, not the nature of the statement; the entrant has no prior right, so he gets the full half. The passage then addresses the case when these statements occur during death illness (marad al-mawt), where the manumission is treated as a will and so is limited to one-third of the estate. The calculation: each slave is divided into four parts; the stayer has three parts free, each of the other two have two parts free, total seven parts of freedom. Since only one-third of the estate can be applied, the shares are proportionally reduced to fit within the third.

Key Terms
الْخَارِجُ (al-khārij): The slave who left after the first statement.
الثَّابِتُ (al-thābit): The slave who remained present throughout both statements.
الدَّاخِلُ (al-dākhil): The slave who entered after the first statement and was present for the second.
الْإِيجَابُ (al-ījāb): The statement that creates the legal effect; here, the declaration of manumission.
مَرَض الْمَوْت (marad al-mawt): Death illness; a condition in which a person is likely to die, and legal dispositions are treated as wills limited to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what fraction of the slave who stayed (al-thabit) becomes free?
A) One-half
B) Three-quarters
C) One-quarter
D) All

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school recognizes internal differences on partial manumission, which are resolved by carefully applying the principle that prior rights affect the distribution of subsequent ambiguous declarations.

Freeing Three Slaves: Consecutive Statements and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and dispute on freedom shares for three slaves with two statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the freedom shares of three slaves with two ambiguous statements and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Prior partial freedom reduces the later share; a new slave gets a full half from the second statement.

This passage examines a scenario where a master makes two ambiguous statements of manumission involving three slaves. The key legal issue is how to distribute freedom shares when the master dies before clarifying his intention. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Imam Muhammad holds the third slave receives one-quarter, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold he receives one-half. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (Sahibayn) on the third slave's share.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Muhammad disagrees; state his ruling for the third slave.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Explain Imam Muhammad's reasoning: the second statement applies equally to the present and the new slave.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explain why Sahibayn argue the third slave has no prior right, so he gets the full half.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: a 1/4 vs 1/2 difference in freedom share affects estate distribution.

Chapter on freeing one of two slaves... So from the first statement half, and from the second a quarter, together three-fourths (3/4) of him is freed. And by this proof: if by the second speech this same slave is meant then his remaining half becomes free. And if by it the incoming third slave (C) is meant then his remaining half is not freed. Therefore half-half was kept, i.e., from the remaining half, half is considered free. So from the second speech a quarter and from the first half together (3/4 three-fourths) became free. And regarding the third slave, Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) says: when the second speech making one slave free occurs between him and the present (A) even though from it he got a quarter, then the third incoming one will also get that much. So in the third, a quarter becomes free. And Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them both) say: that slave indeed comes between them both and each gets half. But for the present one, from half it is reduced to only a quarter because he has already become half-free from the first speech, as we have stated, and the third incoming one had no prior right, so in him the freedom of half is established. Explanation: 'And whoever has three slaves, two come to him and he says: one of you is free, then one leaves and another enters...' The translation clarifies the meaning. 'Then he dies without clarifying...' Benefit: But if he remained alive, he would be ordered to explain his ambiguous statement. If he said regarding the first speech: 'I meant the slave who left,' then that one becomes fully free. But if he said regarding the first speech: 'I meant the slave who remained standing,' then that one becomes free. After the third slave's arrival, if he says the same sentence 'one of you is free,' it is understood that he informed of the freedom of A, because the one present (A) is already free from the first sentence. And if the master first clarifies the second speech saying 'I meant the third slave (C),' then that one becomes free, but then he is questioned about the first speech and whomever he specifies, whether A or B, that one becomes free. And if he means the present one by the second speech, then the one who left is free and the third remains a slave. Now that the master has died, his detail is mentioned here in the text. 'As for the one who left, the first obligation is shared between him and the one who remained, and he is the one to whom the statement was repeated...' The example of this issue: Zaid has three slaves: (1) Aslam (A), (2) Babar (B), (3) Jamal (C). Among them, Aslam (A) and Babar (B) came to him, so he said, 'One of you is free.' Then A remained standing and B left. So by this saying, half of each became free. Then Jamal came, and Zaid again said, 'One of you is free.' So from this sentence, half-freedom came to both A and C. But because half of A was already free from before, he became entitled to only an additional quarter, so three-quarters of him became free, and the remaining each became half-free.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage begins with the chapter on freeing one of two slaves but extends to a scenario with three slaves and two consecutive statements. The author (likely from Ashraf al-Hidayah) explains the ruling when a master says 'one of you is free' to a pair of slaves, then one leaves and another enters, and he repeats the same statement to the new pair. The legal reasoning follows Hanafi methodology: the first statement creates an undetermined freedom shared equally between the two slaves present, so each becomes half-free. When the second statement is made to the new pair (the originally present slave and the newly arrived one), that statement also creates half-freedom shared between them. However, the originally present slave already has half-freedom, so he only gains an additional quarter (since the half from the second statement is shared, giving him a quarter more), totaling three-quarters. The other two slaves become half-free each.

When the master dies before clarifying which slave he intended by each statement, the ruling takes effect as above because the ambiguity cannot be resolved. Imam Muhammad holds that the third slave gets only a quarter, based on the principle that the second statement applies equally to the present slave (who already has prior freedom) and the third, so each should get the same share. But Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the third slave had no prior freedom, so the second statement gives him a full half (shared with the present slave, but the present slave's half is reduced due to overlap). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not stated here explicitly, but the commentary explains both views.

The passage also includes a benefit (fa'idah) about what would happen if the master were alive: he could clarify his ambiguous statements, and the freedom would apply accordingly to the specified slave.

Key Terms
مجمل قول (mujmal qawl): An ambiguous statement that does not specify which slave is intended for freedom.
عتق (itq): Freeing a slave; manumission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario with three slaves, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what share of freedom does the third slave (C) receive?
A) One-quarter (1/4)
B) One-half (1/2)
C) Three-quarters (3/4)
D) Full freedom

Key Takeaway
In ambiguous manumission, prior partial freedom reduces the later share, but a slave with no prior right receives the full share from the statement.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Share Calculation for Manumission in Terminal Illness
Intra-Hanafi calculation of freedom shares in terminal illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Sahibayn and Imam Muhammad's method for calculating freedom shares when a terminally ill master frees multiple slaves, and apply the appropriate method to compute partial freedom.

Core Idea
In marad al-mawt, freeing a slave is a will limited to one-third; Sahibayn and Muhammad differ on the base number of shares.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute on how to calculate the shares of freedom when a terminally ill master frees multiple slaves. Both methods treat the freedom as a will effective only within one-third of the estate, but they differ on the initial computation of freedom shares. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to handle fractional allocations in estate-related manumission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
What is the exact point of disagreement? The base number of shares per slave (7 in Sahibayn vs 6 in Muhammad) and the resulting total estate value (21 vs 18).
2
Understand the Sahibayn's method
They start with freedom shares = 7 (present 3, other two 2 each). Since freedom is limited to one-third, heirs must get double (14). So each slave valued at 7, total 21. Present freed shares = 3, works for 4; others freed shares = 2 each, work for 5.
3
Understand Imam Muhammad's method
He reduces the third slave's share to 1, making freedom shares = 6. Heirs' share doubled = 12, so each slave valued at 6, total 18. Present freed shares = 3, works for 3; other two? The text says 'the rest is as before' but with base 6.
The third slave gets only 1 share freed.
4
Trace the source of the difference
The difference originates from the basic (non-illness) case: Sahibayn assign 1/2 to the third slave; Muhammad assigns 1/4. This changes the total freedom shares.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Different number of shares freed for each slave and different work obligations to earn the remainder for heirs. The present slave is better off under Muhammad (1/2 freed vs 3/7).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آقا اگر مرض الموت میں مذکورہ قول کرے تو کیا حکم ہے
قَالَ فَإِنْ كَانَ الْقَوْلُ مِنْهُ فِى الْمَرَضِ قَسَّمَ التَّلكَ عَلى هَذَا وَشَرْحُ ذَالِكَ اَنْ يُجْمَعَ بَيْنَ سِهَامِ الْعِتْقِ وَهِيَ سَبْعَةٌ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا لِأَنَّا نَجْعَلُ كُلَّ رَقْبَةٍ عَلَى أَرْبَعَةٍ لِحَاجَتِنَا إِلَى ثَلَثَةِ الْأَرْبَاعِ فَنَقُولُ يَعْتِقُ مِنَ الثَّابِتِ ثَلَثَةُ أَسْهُم وَمِنَ الْآخَرَيْنِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا سَهُمَانِ فَيَبْلُغُ سَهَامُ الْعِتْقِ سَبْعَةَ وَالْعِتْقُ فِى مَرَضِ الْمَوْتِ وَصِيَّةٌ وَمَحَلُّ نِفَاذِهَا
التَّلْتُ فَلَابُدَّ اَنْ يُجْعَلَ سِهَامُ الْوَرَثَةِ ضِعْفُ ذَالِكَ فَيُجْعَلُ كُلُّ رَقْبَةٍ عَلَى سَبْعَةٍ وَ جَمِيعُ الْمَالِ اَحَدٌ وَ عِشْرُونَ فَيَعْتِقُ مِنَ الثَّابِتِ ثَلَثَةٌ وَيَسْعَى فِى اَرْبَعَةٍ وَيَعْتِقُ مِنَ الْبَاقِيِينَ مِنْ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا سَهُمَانٍ وَيَسْعَى فِي خَمْسَةٍ فَإِذَا تَأَمَّلْتَ وَجَمَعْتَ اِسْتَقَامَ التَّلْتُ وَ الثَّلُثَانِ وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ يُجْعَلُ كُلُّ رَقْبَةٍ عَلَى سِتَّةٍ لِأَنَّهُ يَعْتِقُ مِنَ الدَّاخِلِ عِنْدَهُ سَهُمْ فَنَقَصَتْ سِهَامُ الْعِتْقِ بِسَهُم وَصَارَ جَمِيعُ الْمَالِ ثَمَانِيَةَ عَشَرَوَبَاقِي التَّخْرِيحِ مَامَرَّ
Master: If the statement from him is in illness, he divides the one-third accordingly. Explanation: That we gather the shares of freedom, which are seven according to their (Sahibayn's) statement, because we make each slave upon four (parts) due to our need for three-fourths. So we say: from the present (slave), three shares become free; from each of the other two, two shares; so the shares of freedom amount to seven. And freeing in terminal illness is a will, and its enforcement is limited to one-third. So it is necessary to make the shares of the heirs double that. So we make each slave upon seven, and all the wealth is twenty-one. So from the present, three are freed, and he works (to earn) for four; from each of the remaining two, two shares are freed, and each works for five. If you reflect and add, the one-third and two-thirds become correct. According to Muhammad, each slave is made upon six, because with him, from the incoming (third) one share is freed, so the shares of freedom decreased by one share, and all the wealth becomes eighteen. The rest of the calculation is as before.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario when the master made the statements during his terminal illness (marad al-mawt). In Hanafi law, freeing a slave during terminal illness is treated as a will (wasiyyah), and thus it is only enforceable within one-third of the estate. The author explains the calculation: according to the two seniors (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), in the original (non-illness) case, the freedom shares were: present slave 3/4, third slave 1/2, and outer slave (who left) 1/2. To compute the shares in terms of units, each slave's total value is divided into four parts (to get three-fourths for one slave). The total freedom shares become 7 (3 for present, 2 for each of the other two). Since only one-third of the estate can be used for these freedoms, the heirs' shares must be twice that amount (2/3). So each slave is valued at 7 shares (since the total estate is the sum of the three slaves), making total estate 21 shares. Then the present slave has 3 freed (from the 7) and must work to earn 4 for the heirs; each of the other two has 2 freed and must work for 5. This satisfies the one-third/two-third division.

Imam Muhammad has a different calculation. In his view, the third slave gets only 1/4 (one share out of four) in the basic case. So the total freedom shares are fewer: only 6 (3 for present, 2 for one, 1 for the third? Actually the text says: 'because with him, from the incoming one share is freed' meaning the third slave gets 1 share out of 6 per slave? Let's read carefully: 'At Muhammad, each slave is made upon six, because with him, from the incoming (third) one share is freed, so the shares of freedom decreased by one share.' So with Imam Muhammad's view, the total freedom shares are 6 instead of 7, so each slave's value is divided into 6 parts, total estate 18. The distribution then adjusts accordingly. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the computation method.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness from which the person dies; acts during this illness are treated like wills.
التَّلْتُ (al-thulth): One-third of the estate that can be willed.
سِهَام (siham): Shares; fractional parts used in calculation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the total number of shares for all three slaves combined in the marad al-mawt calculation?
A) 21
B) 18
C) 7
D) 6

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on share calculation for manumission in marad al-mawt centers on the base number of shares, affecting the proportional freedom of each slave.

Ambiguous Manumission and Automatic Specification
Ruling with proof on automatic specification of the other slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a master makes an ambiguous manumission to two slaves and then one is sold, dies, or made mudabbar, and explain the proof using the principle of specification by elimination.

Core Idea
When one of two ambiguously freed slaves is sold or dies, the other is automatically the freed one.

A master declares 'one of you is free' to two slaves, but does not specify which. What happens if he later sells one, or one dies, or he makes one a mudabbar? The Hanafi school derives a clear rule: the other slave becomes free. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that rule and its applications, including analogous cases in divorce and gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ambiguous Declaration
The master says to two slaves, 'One of you is free.' No specification is made initially.
This is a suspended (mu'allaq) manumission that requires a clarifying event.
2
Recognize Events That Remove a Slave from Eligibility
Death, sale, and tadbir all remove the slave from being a possible recipient of immediate freedom. Death eliminates the slave entirely; sale transfers ownership, removing the master's authority to free; tadbir commits to freedom after death, contradicting immediate freedom.
These events each create a conflict with the initially intended immediate manumission.
3
Apply the Reasoning of Purpose Conflict
Sale seeks price, tadbir retains benefit until death—both contradict the obligated freedom. The other slave is then specified by default (ta'ayyun).
The text gives two parallel reasons: removal of eligibility and contradiction of purpose.
4
Note the Scope: All Transfers of Ownership
The ruling applies to any valid or defective sale, with or without possession, and even to gift, sadaqah, and delivery (tamlik).
According to Abu Yusuf, these are all annexed to sale.
5
Extend to Divorce and Umm Walad
If a man says to his two wives 'one of you is divorced' and one dies, the other is divorced. Similarly, making one slave an umm walad triggers the same specification.
The principle is consistent: removal of one possible recipient specifies the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ قَالَ لِعَبْدَيْهِ اَحَدُ كَمَا حَرَّ فَبَاعَ اَحَدُهُمَا أَوْمَاتَ أَوْقَالَ لَهُ أَنْتَ حُرٌّ بَعْدَ مَوْتِي عَتَقَ الْآخَرُ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَبْقَ مَحَلًّا لِلْعِتْقِ أَصْلًا بِالْمَوْتِ وَلِلْعِتْقِ مِنْ جِهَتِهِ بِالْبَيْعِ وَلِلْعِتْقِ مِنْ كُلِّ وَجْهٍ بِالتَّدْبِيرِ فَتَعَيَّنَ الْآخَرُ وَلِأَنَّهُ بِالْبَيْعِ قَصَدَ الْوُصُولَ إِلَى الثَّمَنِ وَبِالتَّدْبِيرِ إِبْقَاءَ الِانْتِفَاعِ إِلَى مَوْتِهِ وَالْمَقْصِدَانِ يُنَافِيَانِ الْعِتْقَ الْمُلْتَزَمَ فَتَعَيَّنَ لَهُ الْآخَرُ دَلَالَةً وَكَذَا إِذَا اسْتَوْلَدَ أَحَدَهُمَا لِلْمَعْنَيَيْنِ وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ الْبَيْعِ الصَّحِيحِ وَالْفَاسِدِ مَعَ الْقَبْضِ وَبِدُونِهِ وَالْمُطْلَقِ وَبِشَرْطِ الْخِيَارِ لِأَحَدِ الْمُتَعَاقِدَيْنِ لِإِطْلَاقِ جَوَابِ الْكِتَابِ وَالْمَعْنَى مَا قُلْنَا وَالْعَرْضُ عَلَى الْبَيْعِ مُلْحَقٌ بِهِ فِي الْمَحْفُوظِ عَنْ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَالْهِبَةُ وَالتَّسْلِيمُ وَالصَّدَقَةُ وَالتَّسْلِيمُ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْبَيْعِ لِأَنَّهُ تَمْلِيكٌ وَكَذَلِكَ لَوْ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتَيْهِ إِحْدَاكُمَا طَالِقٌ ثُمَّ مَاتَتْ إِحْدَاهُمَا لِمَا قُلْنَا وَكَذَا لَوْ وَطِئَ أَحَدَهُمَا لِمَا نُبَيِّنُ
And whoever says to his two slaves, 'One of you is free,' then sells one of them, or one dies, or he says to him, 'You are free after my death,' the other becomes free. Because he no longer remains a place for freeing: originally due to death, and due to freeing from his side by sale, and due to freeing from every aspect by tadbir. So the other becomes specified. And because by sale he intended to obtain the price, and by tadbir he intended to retain benefit until his death, and both purposes contradict the obligated freedom, so the other becomes specified for it by indication. Likewise if he makes one of them an umm walad here, for both meanings. And there is no difference between a valid sale and a defective sale, whether with or without possession, whether absolute or with a condition of option for one of the contracting parties, due to the generality of the book's answer and the meaning we stated. And offering for sale is annexed to it according to what is preserved from Abu Yusuf. And gift, delivery, and charity are like sale because it is a transfer of ownership. And similarly if he says to his two wives, 'One of you is divorced,' then one of them dies, for what we said. And similarly if he has intercourse with one of them, as we will explain.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah states a ruling on ambiguous manumission. If a master says 'one of you is free' to two slaves, and then one of the slaves dies, is sold, or is made a mudabbar (free after master's death), the other slave automatically becomes the one who is free. The proof is that the first slave is no longer eligible for freedom: by death completely, by sale because the master has transferred ownership and thus cannot free him, by tadbir because the master has committed to freeing him after death, which is a different kind of freedom. Both sale and tadbir contradict the initially intended immediate freedom, so the other slave is specifically indicated. The ruling also applies if the master makes one an umm walad (mother of his child). All types of sale (valid or defective, with or without possession, with or without option) are treated the same. Gift, delivery, sadaqah are also like sale because they transfer ownership. The same principle applies to divorce: if a man says to his two wives 'one of you is divorced' and one dies, the other is divorced by analogy.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Manumission; the freeing of a slave from bondage.
التدبير (al-tadbir): A declaration that a slave will be free after the master's death.
البيع (al-bay'): Sale; transfer of ownership for a price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says to his two slaves 'one of you is free' and then sells one of them, what is the ruling?
A) The sold slave becomes free
B) The other slave becomes free
C) Both become free
D) The sale is invalid

Key Takeaway
When one of two ambiguously freed slaves is removed by sale, death, or tadbir, the other is automatically specified as the freed one.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Ambiguous Manumission and Intercourse
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether intercourse specifies which slave becomes free after an ambiguous manumission, and apply the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a slave woman does not imply retention of ownership, unlike a wife, because the purpose differs.

This passage from Al-Hidayah examines a subtle dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The question is whether a master's act of intercourse with one of two female slaves, after declaring 'one of you is free,' serves to clarify which one remains enslaved. The disagreement hinges on the purpose of intercourse with a slave versus a wife—a distinction that leads to different rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the question
A master has two female slaves and says 'one of you is free,' then has intercourse with one. Does the other slave become free?
The dispute is about the status of the slave not slept with.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that the other does not become free; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that she does become free.
Both agree that the slave with whom intercourse occurred remains a slave.
3
Trace the Sahibayn's proof: analogy to divorce
They analogize to the case of ambiguous divorce: if a man says 'one of you is divorced' to his two wives and then has intercourse with one, that wife remains married and the other becomes divorced. Here, intercourse shows retention of ownership in the one slept with, so the other loses ownership (becomes free).
The analogy is based on the principle that intercourse is only lawful within ownership or marriage.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's rebuttal: distinguishing slave women from wives
Abu Hanifa argues that the primary purpose of marriage is offspring, so intercourse indicates desire to retain the wife for that purpose. With slaves, the purpose is mere gratification, so intercourse does not imply retention.
Thus, the analogy fails; the slave with whom he slept remains a slave, and the other is not specified as free.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The text states that Abu Hanifa's view is 'not given fatwa upon,' meaning the view of the Sahibayn is the relied-upon position.
Practically, the other slave becomes free at the moment of intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ لِأَمَتَيْهِ إِحْدَاكُمَا حُرَّةٌ ثُمَّ جَامَعَ أَحَدَهُمَا لَمْ يَعْتِقِ الْأُخْرَى عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَعْتِقُ لِأَنَّ الْوَطْءَ لَا يَحِلُّ إِلَّا فِي الْمِلْكِ فَكَانَ بِالْوَطْءِ مُسْتَبْقِيًا الْمِلْكَ فِي الْمَوْطُوءَةِ فَتَعَيَّنَتِ الْأُخْرَى لِزَوَالِهِ بِالْعِتْقِ كَمَا فِي الطَّلَاقِ وَلَهُ أَنَّ الْمِلْكَ قَائِمٌ فِي الْمَوْطُوءَةِ لِأَنَّ الْعَقْدَ فِي الْمُنْكَرَةِ وَهِيَ مُعَيَّنَةٌ فَكَانَ وَطْؤُهَا حَلَالًا فَلَا يُجْعَلُ بَيَانًا وَلِهَذَا حَلَّ وَطْؤُهُمَا عَلَى مَذْهَبِهِ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ لَا يُفْتَى بِهِ ثُمَّ يُقَالُ الْعِتْقُ غَيْرُ نَازِلٍ قَبْلَ الْبَيَانِ لِتَعَلُّقِهِ بِهِ أَوْ يُقَالُ نَازِلٌ فِي الْمُنْكَرَةِ فَيَظْهَرُ فِي حَقِّ مَنْ تَقْبَلُهُ وَالْوَطْءُ يُصَادِقُ الْمُعَيَّنَةَ بِخِلَافِ الطَّلَاقِ لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ الْأَصْلِيَّ مِنَ النِّكَاحِ الْوَلَدُ وَقَصْدُ الْوَلَدِ بِالْوَطْءِ يَدُلُّ عَلَى اسْتِبْقَاءِ الْمِلْكِ فِي الْمَوْطُوءَةِ صِيَانَةً لِلْوَلَدِ أَمَّا الْأَمَةُ فَالْمَقْصُودُ مِنْ وَطْئِهَا قَضَاءُ الشَّهْوَةِ دُونَ الْوَلَدِ فَلَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى الِاسْتِبْقَاءِ
And if he says to his two female slaves, 'One of you is free,' then has intercourse with one of them, the other does not become free according to Abu Hanifa. But they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say she becomes free, because intercourse is only lawful in ownership, so by intercourse he intended to retain ownership in the one with whom he had intercourse, so the other becomes specified for freedom to cease, as in divorce. And his proof is that ownership remains in the one with whom he had intercourse, because the contract was in the unspecified one, and she is specific, so his intercourse with her is lawful, so it is not made a clarification. Therefore, intercourse with both is permissible in his madhhab, except that it is not given fatwa upon. Then it is said either that freedom does not descend before clarification because it is contingent upon it, or it is said that it descends in the unspecified one and becomes apparent in the one who accepts it. Intercourse confirms the specific, unlike divorce, because the primary purpose of marriage is offspring, and the intention of offspring through intercourse indicates retention of ownership in the one with whom intercourse occurs, for the sake of the child. As for a slave woman, the purpose of intercourse with her is gratification of desire, not offspring, so it does not indicate retention.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ambiguous manumission of two female slaves. If a master says 'one of you is free' and then has intercourse with one of them, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the other slave becomes free, analogizing to the case of divorce where intercourse with one wife after an ambiguous divorce specifies her as undivorced. Abu Hanifa disagrees, arguing that because the contract of sale or the initial statement was ambiguous and the one with whom he had intercourse is a specific individual, the intercourse does not serve as a clarification. He permits intercourse with both, though this is not followed in fatwa. The essential difference is that in marriage, the purpose of intercourse includes having children, which implies retaining the wife in marriage. With slave women, the purpose is pure gratification, so intercourse does not imply a desire to keep her in ownership.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): Female slave.
الوطء (al-wat'): Sexual intercourse.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership; the master's right over his slave.
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of a marriage.
البيان (al-bayan): Clarification; an act that removes ambiguity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn in this passage, why does intercourse with one female slave specify the other as free?
A) Because intercourse is only lawful if she is free
B) Because intercourse shows he intends to keep the other in slavery
C) Because of analogy to divorce
D) Because he cannot have intercourse with a slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers the view that intercourse after an ambiguous manumission specifies the other slave as free, by analogy to divorce, because the purpose of intercourse with slaves is gratification, not offspring.
