Both Partners Claim Paternity from Jointly-Owned Slave Woman
Ruling when both partners claim paternity and the inter-madhhab dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both partners claim paternity from a jointly-owned slave woman and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the Companions' practice as proof.

Core Idea
When both partners claim paternity, Hanafis establish the child's lineage from both with special inheritance, rejecting the qa'if because of Umar's Companions-attested letter.

This passage presents a nuanced Hanafi ruling regarding paternity when two joint owners of a slave woman both claim the child. The school establishes lineage from both, with a unique inheritance arrangement, and contrasts this with the Shafi'i approach that would refer the matter to a physiognomist (qa'if). We will explore the proofs on both sides and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: paternity is established from both partners, the child inherits from them and they inherit from him, but the child's inheritance passes to the survivor first.
The text says 'he is the son of both, he inherits from them and they inherit from him, and he is for the survivor of them.'
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since a child cannot have two biological fathers, we must use a qa'if to determine resemblance. He cites the hadith of Usamah where the Prophet was pleased with a qa'if's statement.
Shafi'i's rational argument: 'the child is not created from two men.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond by citing the letter of Umar to Shurayh, instructing to obscure the matter and rule that the child is the son of both, done in the presence of the Companions. This indicates consensus and establishes the ruling as general, while the Usamah hadith is specific and not generalizable.
The Hanafi proof is dalil naqli (transmitted evidence).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Hanafi, the child inherits from both partners (first from the predeceased, then from the survivor). Under Shafi'i, the qa'if's opinion determines one father, and the other partner has no claim.
This affects inheritance and lineage rights.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because it is based on the practice of the Companions, which is a stronger source than a single hadith that may have been specific to that situation.
The letter of Umar and Ali's similar ruling provide corroboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ إِنِ ادَّعَيَاهُ مَعَاثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُمَا مَعْنَاهُ إِذَا حَمَلَتْ عَلَى مِلْكِهِمَا وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يُرْجَعُ إِلَى قَوْلِ الْقَافَّةِ لِأَنَّ إِثْبَاتَ النَّسَبِ مِنْ شَحْصَيْنِ مَعَ عِلْمِنَا أَنَّ الْوَلَدَلَا يَنْخَلِقُ مِنْ مَاتَيْنِ مُتَعَدَّرٌ فَعَمَلُنَا بِالشُّبْهِ وَقَدْ سَرَّرَسُولُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ بِقَوْلِ الْقَائِفِ فِي أَسَامَةَ وَلَنَا كِتَابُ عُمَرُ إِلَى شُرَيْحٍ فِى هَذِهِ الْحَادِثَةِ لَبَّسَافَلَيْسَ عَلَيْهِمَا وَلَوْ بَيَّنَّا لَبَيِّنَ لَهُمَا وَهُوَ ابْنُهُمَا يَرِثُهُمَا وَ يَرِثَانِهِ وَهُوَ لِلْبَاقِي مِنْهُمَا وَ كَانَ ذَالِكَ بِمَحْضَرٍ مِنَ الصَّحَابَةِ وَعَنْ عَلِيَّ مِثْلُ ذَالِكَ
And if both claim him together, his lineage becomes established from both of them. This is when she became pregnant while in their joint ownership. And Al-Shafi'i said: One should resort to the statement of the physiognomist (qa'if), because establishing lineage from two persons while we know that a child is not created from two men is impossible, so we act on resemblance. And the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was pleased with the statement of the physiognomist regarding Usamah. And we have the letter of Umar to Shurayh regarding this incident: 'Obscure it, so it is not upon them, and if you had clarified, you would have clarified for them.' And he is the son of both, he inherits from them and they inherit from him, and he is (in terms of inheritance) for the survivor of them. And that was in the presence of the Companions. And from Ali similar to that.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where both partners jointly claim paternity of the child. The Hanafi position is that paternity is established from both, meaning the child is legally the son of both, with mutual inheritance rights, but physically it is only possible from one; the child's lineage is suspended until one partner dies, at which point the child inherits from the deceased and then from the survivor. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that since a child cannot have two biological fathers, we must rely on a physiognomist (qa'if) to determine resemblance and attribute the child to one father. The Hanafi response cites the practice of Umar and Ali, who in a similar case ruled that the child belongs to both with the inheritance arrangement, without resorting to a qa'if. The hadith about Usamah is used by Shafi'i to support the qa'if's authority, but Hanafis interpret it differently: the Prophet was pleased with the qa'if's statement in that specific case, but it does not establish a general rule. The Hanafi proof is based on the letter of Umar to Shurayh, which instructed to leave the matter ambiguous and rule that the child is the son of both, and this was done in the presence of the Companions, indicating consensus.

Key Terms
قائف (qa'if): A person skilled in physiognomy (qiyafah) who claims the ability to determine lineage by physical resemblance.
تشبيه (tashbih): Resemblance; here it refers to using physical resemblance as evidence for lineage, as proposed by Shafi'i.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when both partners claim paternity of a child from a jointly-owned slave woman, what happens to the child's inheritance?
A) The child inherits from both partners equally immediately
B) The child inherits from the survivor, and the survivor inherits from the child
C) The child inherits from neither
D) The child only inherits from the partner who resembles him

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when both partners claim paternity from a jointly-owned slave woman, the child's lineage is established from both, with a specific inheritance arrangement, and the court does not resort to a qa'if.

Master's Intercourse with Mukatab's Slave-Girl: Rulings on Child, Payments, and Lineage
Ruling on child from master and mukatab's slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for a child born from a master and his mukatab's slave-girl, including the conditions for lineage, the payments required, and the effect on the slave-girl's status.

Core Idea
A child born from master and mukatab's slave-girl is free upon payment of value; lineage requires mukatab's confirmation.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a unique scenario: a master has intercourse with a slave-girl owned by his mukatab (a slave under a manumission contract). The master claims the child as his own. The rulings here balance the master's rights over the mukatab's earnings with the mukatab's ownership rights. You will learn the legal consequences, including the payments of 'uqr and qimat, and the critical importance of the mukatab's verification for establishing lineage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal issue
The scenario involves a master and his mukatab's slave-girl. The master claims the child. Determine what legal questions arise: Is the child free or enslaved? Is lineage established? What must the master pay? Does the slave-girl become umm walad?
2
Examine the master's right over mukatab's earnings
The passage states the master cannot dispose of the mukatab's earnings. Contrast with a father who can take his son's earnings. This limitation is key: the master lacks full ownership over the mukatab's property, so his claim to the child is not automatic.
The father analogy is used to show that the son's verification (tasdiq) is not needed because the father owns his son's earnings. Here the master does not own the mukatab's earnings, so the mukatab's verification is needed.
3
Analyze the payments: 'uqr and qimat
The master must pay 'uqr (dower) for the slave-girl because his ownership did not precede intercourse. He must also pay qimat (market value of the child) to free the child, analogized to the case of the maghrur (deceived person). Understand why both payments are required.
The child is freed upon payment of qimat, similar to the maghrur who, after paying the child's value to the true owner, frees the child.
4
Understand the condition for lineage: tasdiq of mukatab
The child's lineage is established from the master only if the mukatab confirms the paternity. This is because the master lacks real ownership over the slave-girl, so his claim alone is insufficient. The mukatab's right must be respected.
If the mukatab denies, lineage is not established. However, if the master later comes to own the child (e.g., after the mukatab's freedom or death), lineage is established because the impediment (the mukatab's right) ceases.
5
Note the slave-girl's status: not umm walad
Even though the child is the master's free child, the slave-girl does not become umm walad (mother of a free child) because the master has no real ownership over her at the time of intercourse. The same applies in the maghrur case.
This distinction is important: umm walad status is tied to ownership. Without ownership, the slave-girl remains a mere object of 'uqr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا يَمْلِكُ التَّصَرُّفَ فِي إِكْسَابِ مُكَاتِبِهِ حَتَّى لَا يَتَمَلَّكَهُ وَالْابُ يَمْلِكُ تَمَلُّكَهُ فَلَا مُعْتَبَرَ بِتَصْدِيقِ الْإِبْنِ وَعَلَيْهِ عُقْرُهَا لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَتَقَدَّمُهُ الْمِلْكَ لِاَنَّ مَالَهُ مِنَ الْحَقِّ كَافِ لِصِحَّةِ الْإِسْتِيلادِ لِمَانَذْ كُرُهُ وَقِيْمَةً وَلَدِهَا لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى الْمَغْرُورٍ حَيْثُ اعْتَمَدَ دَلِيْلًا وَهُوَ أَنَّهُ كَسْبُ كَسْبِهِ فَلَمْ يَرْضَ بِرقِهِ فَيَكُونُ حُرًّا بِالْقِيِّمَةِ ثَابِتُ النَّسَبِ مِنْهُ وَلَا تَصِيرُ الْجَارِيَةُ أمَّ وَلَدٍ لَهُ لأَنَّهُ لَا مِلْكَ لَهُ فِيْهَا حَقِيقَةٌ كَمَافِي وَلَدِ الْمَغْرُورِ وَإِنْ كَذَّبَهُ الْمُكَاتَبُ فِي النَّسَب لَمْ يَثبُتْ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَصْدِيقِهِ فَلَوْ مَلَكَهُ يَوْمَا ثَبَتَ نَسَبُهُ مِنْهُ لِقِيَامِ الْمُوْجِبِ وَ زَوَالِ حَقِ الْمُكَاتَبِ اذْهُوَ الْمَانِعُ
He does not have the right to dispose of his mukatab's earnings, so he cannot take possession of it. But the father has the right to take possession of it, so the son's verification is not considered. And he owes her 'uqr because his ownership does not precede hers, because his right (the master's) is sufficient for the validity of istilad, as we mentioned. And the value of her child, because he is like the deceived one (maghrur) since he relied on a proof, namely that this is the earning of his own earning, so he is not content with his enslavement, so the child becomes free by paying the value, and his lineage is established from him. And the slave-girl does not become umm walad for him because he has no real ownership over her, as in the case of the child of the maghrur. And if the mukatab denies the paternity, it is not established, as we explained that his verification is necessary. But if he later owns him (the child) one day, his lineage is established from him because the cause exists and the mukatab's right has ceased, as that was the impediment.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a master has intercourse with his mukatab's slave-girl and claims the resulting child. According to Hanafi fiqh, the child's lineage is established from the master only if the mukatab confirms (tasdiq) the master's claim. This is because the master lacks full ownership rights over the mukatab's property; he cannot dispose of the mukatab's earnings. In contrast, a father can take his son's earnings, so the son's verification is not needed. The master must pay the 'uqr (proper dower) for the slave-girl because his ownership did not precede the intercourse. He must also pay the child's value (qimat) because the master is considered deceived (maghrur) — he relied on the apparent fact that the child is his own earning (since the mukatab's earnings are effectively his), so he is not willing to see the child remain enslaved. Thus, the child becomes free upon payment of his value, and lineage is established to the master. However, the slave-girl does not become an umm walad (mother of a child) for him because he has no real ownership over her. If the mukatab denies the paternity, the lineage is not established. But if later the master comes to own the child (e.g., after the mukatab's freedom or death), then the lineage is established because the impediment (the mukatab's right) has ceased.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has made a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, allowing him to earn and purchase his freedom.
عقر (ʿuqr): The proper dower (mahr) owed for a slave-girl when intercourse occurs without valid ownership.
قيمة (qīmah): The market value of the child, which must be paid by the master to the mukatab to free the child.
مغرور (maghrūr): One who is deceived, here referring to the master who relied on a false impression that the child was his own earning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what must the master pay when he claims a child from his mukatab's slave-girl?
A) Only the 'uqr (dower)
B) Only the qimat (child's value)
C) Both the 'uqr and the qimat
D) Nothing, if the mukatab confirms the nasab

Key Takeaway
The child is freed upon payment of its value, lineage requires the mukatab's confirmation, and the slave-girl does not become umm walad.

Yamin Ghamus: Deliberate False Oath and the Dispute over Kaffarah
Definition and ruling of yamin ghamus with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that yamin ghamus does not require kaffarah and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A deliberate false oath on a past event is a major sin expiated only by repentance, not by kaffarah.

Oaths in Islamic law are classified into three types: yamin ghamus (false past oath), yamin mun'aqida (future oath), and yamin laghw (heedless oath). This lesson focuses on the first type, analyzing its ruling in the Hanafi school and the reasoning behind the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i over whether kaffarah (expiation) is required.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on yamin ghamus according to the Hanafi school: it is a major sin (kabirah) that requires repentance and seeking forgiveness, but no kaffarah.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kaffarah is obligatory for yamin ghamus because it violates the sanctity of Allah's name.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i analogy: since kaffarah is legislated to remove the sin of violating Allah's name, and yamin ghamus involves such violation, it resembles yamin mun'aqida (broken future oath) which requires kaffarah.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the key distinction: yamin ghamus is a 'pure major sin' (kabirah mahdah) where the sin is inherent, whereas yamin mun'aqida is permissible, and sin only arises from breaking it. Kaffarah, an act of worship requiring intention (niyyah), cannot be attached to an inherent sin.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: In the Hanafi school, a person who commits yamin ghamus only needs to repent; in the Shafi'i school, they must also perform kaffarah (e.g., feed ten poor people, fast three days, or free a slave).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ الْأَيْمَانُ ثَلَثَةُ أَضْرُبِ الْيَمِينُ الْغَمُوسُ وَيَمِيْنٌ مُنْعَقِدَةٌ وَيَمِيْنَ لَغَوّ فَالْغَمُوسُ هُوَ الْحَلْفُ عَلَى أَمْرِمَاضِ يَعْتَمِدُ الْكِذْبَ فِيهِ فَهَذِهِ الْيَمِينُ يَأْثَمُ فِيهَا صَاحِبُهَا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ حَلَفَ كَاذِبًا اَدْخَلَهُ اللَّهُ النَّارَوَ لَا كَفَّارَةَ فِيْهَا إِلَّا التَّوْبَةُ وَالْإِسْتِغْفَارُ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ فِيهَا الْكَفَّارَةُ لِأَنَّهَا شُرِعَتْ لِرَفْعِ ذَنْبِ هَتْكِ حُرْمَةِ اسْمِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَقَدْ تَحَقَّقَ بِالْإِسْتِشْهَادِ بِاللَّهِ كَاذِبًا فَاشْبَهَ الْمَعْقُودَةَ وَلَنَا أَنَّهَا كَبِيرَةٌ مَحْضَةٌ وَالْكَفَّارَةُ عِبَادَةٌ تَتَأَذَى الصَّوْمِ وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيهَا البَيَّةُ فَلَاتُنَاطُ بِهَا بِخِلَافِ الْمَعْقُودَةِ لِاَنَّهَا مُبَاحَةٌ وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيْهَا ذَنْبٌ فَهُوَ مُتَاخِرٌ مُتَعَلَّقٌ بِاخْتِيَارِ مُبْتَدٍ وَمَا فِي الْغَمُوسِ مُلَازِمٌ فَيَمْتَنِعُ الْإِلْحَاقِ
He said: Oaths are of three types: the yamin ghamus, the yamin mun'aqida, and the yamin laghw. The ghamus is the oath on a past matter in which one intends falsehood. This oath makes its taker sinful because of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever swears a false oath, Allah will enter him into the Fire.' There is no expiation (kaffarah) in it except repentance and seeking forgiveness. Al-Shafi'i said: There is kaffarah in it because kaffarah was legislated to remove the sin of violating the sanctity of Allah's name, and that has occurred by falsely invoking Allah as witness, so it resembles the mun'aqida oath. Our proof is that it is a pure major sin, and kaffarah is an act of worship that is fulfilled by fasting, and intention is required in it, so it cannot be attached to it (the sin), unlike the mun'aqida oath because it is permissible, and if there is sin in it, it is subsequent, related to a new choice, whereas in the ghamus it is inherent, so analogy (ilhaq) is prevented.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces the classification of oaths into three categories and focuses on the yamin ghamus. The yamin ghamus is a deliberate false oath about a past event. It is a major sin (kabirah) according to Hanafi fiqh, as established by the hadith. The Hanafi school holds that no expiation (kaffarah) is required for this oath; only sincere repentance (tawbah) and seeking forgiveness (istighfar) are necessary. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that kaffarah is obligatory because the oath involves using Allah's name falsely, similar to a broken oath (yamin mun'aqida). The Hanafi response is that yamin ghamus is a pure major sin, and kaffarah is an act of worship that requires intention (niyyah) and cannot be attached to an inherent sin. In contrast, the yamin mun'aqida is a permissible oath about the future; if broken, the sin is subsequent and not inherent, making kaffarah appropriate. Thus, the two types are fundamentally different, and the analogy proposed by al-Shafi'i is rejected.

Key Terms
يمين غموس (yamīn ghamūs): A deliberate false oath about a past event, considered a major sin requiring repentance, not kaffarah, in Hanafi fiqh.
يمين منعقدة (yamīn munʿaqidah): An oath about a future action, considered 'bound' or 'contracted'; if broken, kaffarah is required.
يمين لغو (yamīn laghw): A heedless oath without intention, e.g., swearing by mistake or in casual speech; no kaffarah and no sin.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath (yamin mun'aqida) or for certain sins; in ghamus, Hanafi says no kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the required action for a person who commits yamin ghamus?
A) He must pay kaffarah by feeding ten poor people.
B) He must fast three days.
C) He must repent and seek forgiveness; no kaffarah.
D) He must free a slave.

Key Takeaway
Yamin ghamus is a major sin that repentance alone can expiate; kaffarah is reserved for broken permissible oaths about the future.

The False Oath (Al-Yamin Al-Ghamus): A Major Sin Without Expiation
Defines al-yamin al-ghamus and presents Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-yamin al-ghamus from al-yamin al-mun'aqida and state the Hanafi ruling that no kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
A deliberately false oath on a past event is a major sin; only repentance removes it, not expiation.

In this passage, the author classifies oaths into three types and focuses on al-yamin al-ghamus — an oath about a past event sworn with deliberate falsehood. The Hanafi school holds that this is a major sin (kabirah) for which no expiation (kaffarah) is valid; only sincere repentance and seeking forgiveness can atone. Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that kaffarah must be performed because the oath violates the sanctity of Allah's name. Let us examine the evidence and reasoning that support the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Define al-yamin al-ghamus
The passage defines it as swearing about a past matter with deliberate falsehood. Distinguish it from al-yamin al-mun'aqida (future oath) and al-yamin al-laghw (unintentional).
This classification is key to understanding the dispute.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi position: the oath is haram and a major sin (kabirah); no kaffarah is required; only tawba (repentance) and istighfar (seeking forgiveness) are needed.
The ruling is supported by a hadith threatening the Fire.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that kaffarah should apply because the oath violates the sanctity of Allah's name, similar to al-yamin al-mun'aqida.
His proof is qiyas (analogy) between ghamus and mun'aqida.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response: ghamus is a pure major sin; kaffarah is an act of worship requiring intention and cannot remove an inherent sin. In mun'aqida, the sin is contingent on a later choice, so kaffarah can expiate it.
This is a methodological difference in the nature and scope of kaffarah.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: a Hanafi only repents; a Shafi'i must perform kaffarah (free a slave, feed ten poor, or fast three days) in addition to repentance.
This affects actual practice for the layperson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ الْأَيْمَانُ ثَلَثَةُ أَضْرُبِ الْيَمِينُ الْغَمُوسُ وَيَمِينٌ مُنْعَقِدَةٌ وَيَمِينَ لَغَوّ فَالْغَمُوسُ هُوَ الْحَلْفُ عَلَى أَمْرٍ مَاضٍ يَعْتَمِدُ الْكِذْبَ فِيهِ فَهَذِهِ الْيَمِينُ يَأْثَمُ فِيهَا صَاحِبُهَا لِقَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ مَنْ حَلَفَ كَاذِبًا أَدْخَلَهُ اللَّهُ النَّارَ وَلَا كَفَّارَةَ فِيهَا إِلَّا التَّوْبَةُ وَالْإِسْتِغْفَارُ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ فِيهَا الْكَفَّارَةُ لِأَنَّهَا شُرِعَتْ لِرَفْعِ ذَنْبِ هَتْكِ حُرْمَةِ اسْمِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَقَدْ تَحَقَّقَ بِالْإِسْتِشْهَادِ بِاللَّهِ كَاذِبًا فَأَشْبَهَتِ الْمَعْقُودَةَ وَلَنَا أَنَّهَا كَبِيرَةٌ مَحْضَةٌ وَالْكَفَّارَةُ عِبَادَةٌ تُؤَدَّى بِالصَّوْمِ وَيُشْتَرَطُ فِيهَا النِّيَّةُ فَلَا تُنَاطُ بِهَا بِخِلَافِ الْمَعْقُودَةِ لِأَنَّهَا مُبَاحَةٌ وَلَوْ كَانَ فِيهَا ذَنْبٌ فَهُوَ مُتَأَخِّرٌ مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِاخْتِيَارِ مُبْتَدٍ وَمَا فِي الْغَمُوسِ مُلَازِمٌ فَيَمْتَنِعُ الْإِلْحَاقُ
He said: Oaths are of three types: al-yamin al-ghamus, al-yamin al-mun'aqida, and al-yamin al-laghw. Al-ghamus is swearing about a past matter intending falsehood in it. This oath makes its swearer sinful due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever swears falsely, Allah will enter him into the Fire.' There is no expiation in it except repentance and seeking forgiveness. Al-Shafi'i said: Expiation is required in it because expiation was legislated to remove the sin of violating the sanctity of Allah's name, and that is realized by testifying by Allah falsely, so it resembles the mun'aqida. Our proof is that it is a pure major sin, and expiation is an act of worship performed by fasting, and intention is required in it, so it is not attached to it. Unlike mun'aqida because it is permissible, and if there is sin in it, it is subsequent, related to a new choice, whereas in ghamus it is inherent, so analogy is prevented.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi position on al-yamin al-ghamus, the false oath about a past event. The author first defines it as an oath on a past matter with deliberate falsehood. The swearer commits a major sin as indicated by the hadith. The Hanafi ruling is that no expiation (kaffarah) is required, only sincere repentance (tawba) and seeking forgiveness (istighfar). Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that expiation is necessary because it involves disrespect of Allah's name, similar to an executory oath (mun'aqida). The Hanafi response distinguishes ghamus as a pure major sin, whereas expiation is an act of worship requiring intention, so it cannot be yoked to a major sin. Furthermore, in mun'aqida, the sin (if any) arises from a later choice to break the oath, so expiation can remove it. In ghamus, the falsehood is inherent from the start, so expiation is insufficient.

Key Terms
اليَمِينُ الغَمُوسُ (al-yamin al-ghamus): An oath deliberately sworn falsely about a past event.
اليَمِينُ المُنْعَقِدَةُ (al-yamin al-mun'aqida): An executory oath about a future action, whose breach incurs kaffarah.
الكَفَّارَةُ (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath, consisting of freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for one who commits al-yamin al-ghamus?
A) Kaffarah of freeing a slave or fasting three days.
B) Only sincere repentance and seeking forgiveness.
C) Both kaffarah and repentance.
D) Nothing, as it is not sinful.

Key Takeaway
Al-yamin al-ghamus is a major sin for which only repentance suffices; kaffarah is neither required nor effective.
